	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Genealogy</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the study of family lineage and history. For the philosophical technique developed by Nietzsche and Foucault, see <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_(philosophy)" title="Genealogy (philosophy)">Genealogy (philosophy)</a>. For the medical family history, see <a href="/wiki/Family_history_(medicine)" title="Family history (medicine)">Family history (medicine)</a>. For the sociocultural evolution of kinship, see <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_family" title="History of the family">History of the family</a>. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Genealogy (disambiguation)">Genealogy (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:900-158_Ahnentafel_Herzog_Ludwig.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/900-158_Ahnentafel_Herzog_Ludwig.jpg/310px-900-158_Ahnentafel_Herzog_Ludwig.jpg" decoding="async" width="310" height="246" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/900-158_Ahnentafel_Herzog_Ludwig.jpg/465px-900-158_Ahnentafel_Herzog_Ludwig.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/900-158_Ahnentafel_Herzog_Ludwig.jpg/620px-900-158_Ahnentafel_Herzog_Ludwig.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3368" data-file-height="2678" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:900-158_Ahnentafel_Herzog_Ludwig.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The family tree of <a href="/wiki/Louis_III,_Duke_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Louis III, Duke of Württemberg">Ludwig Herzog von</a> <a href="/wiki/W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Württemberg">Württemberg</a> (ruled 1568–1593)</div></div></div>
<p><b>Genealogy</b> (from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">γενεαλογία</span> <i><i lang="el-Latn" title="Greek-language romanization">genealogia</i></i> "the making of a pedigree")<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> is the study of <a href="/wiki/Family" title="Family">families</a>, family history, and the tracing of their <a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">lineages</a>. Genealogists use oral interviews, historical records, genetic analysis, and other records to obtain information about a family and to demonstrate <a href="/wiki/Kinship" title="Kinship">kinship</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pedigree_chart" title="Pedigree chart">pedigrees</a> of its members. The results are often displayed in charts or written as narratives. Although generally used interchangeably, the traditional definition of "genealogy" begins with a person who is usually deceased and traces his or her descendants forward in time, whereas, "family history" begins with a person who is usually living and traces his or her ancestors.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Both the <a href="/wiki/National_Genealogical_Society" title="National Genealogical Society">National Genealogical Society</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Genealogists" title="Society of Genealogists">Society of Genealogists</a> in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> state that the word "genealogy" often refers to the scholarly discipline of researching lineages and connecting generations, whereas "family history" often refers to biographical studies of ones family, including family narratives and traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sog_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sog-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The pursuit of family history and origins tends to be shaped by several motives, including the desire to carve out a place for one's family in the larger historical picture, a sense of responsibility to preserve the past for future generations, and self-satisfaction in accurate storytelling.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Genealogy research is also performed for scholarly or <a href="/wiki/Forensic_genealogy" title="Forensic genealogy">forensic</a> purposes.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Family_tree_indian.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Family_tree_indian.jpg/310px-Family_tree_indian.jpg" decoding="async" width="310" height="526" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d2/Family_tree_indian.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="407" data-file-height="690" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Family_tree_indian.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>12 generations patrilineage of a Hindu Lingayat male from central <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a> spanning over 275 years, depicted in descending order</div></div></div>
<p>Amateur genealogists typically pursue their own ancestry and that of their spouses. Professional genealogists may also conduct research for others, publish books on genealogical methods, teach, or produce their own databases. They may work for companies that provide software or produce materials of use to other professionals and to amateurs. Both try to understand not just where and when people lived, but also their lifestyles, biographies, and motivations. This often requires—or leads to—knowledge of antiquated laws, old political boundaries, migration trends, and historical socioeconomic or religious conditions.
</p><p>Genealogists sometimes specialize in a particular group, e.g. a <a href="/wiki/Scottish_clan" title="Scottish clan">Scottish clan</a>; a particular <a href="/wiki/Surname" title="Surname">surname</a>, such as in a <a href="/wiki/One-name_study" title="One-name study">one-name study</a>; a small community, e.g. a single village or parish, such as in a <a href="/wiki/One-place_study" title="One-place study">one-place study</a>; or a particular, often famous, person. <a href="/wiki/Bloodlines_of_Salem" title="Bloodlines of Salem">Bloodlines of Salem</a> is an example of a specialized family-history group. It welcomes members who can prove descent from a participant of the Salem Witch Trials or who simply choose to support the group.
</p><p>Genealogists and family historians often join <a href="/wiki/Family_history_society" title="Family history society">family history societies</a>, where novices can learn from more experienced researchers. Such societies generally serve a specific geographical area. Their members may also index records to make them more accessible, and engage in advocacy and other efforts to preserve public records and cemeteries. Some schools engage students in such projects as a means to reinforce lessons regarding immigration and history.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Other benefits include family medical histories with families with serious medical conditions that are hereditary.
</p><p>The terms "genealogy" and "family history" are often used synonymously, but some offer a slight difference in definition. The <a href="/wiki/Society_of_Genealogists" title="Society of Genealogists">Society of Genealogists</a>, while also using the terms interchangeably, describes genealogy as the "establishment of a Pedigree by extracting evidence, from valid sources, of how one generation is connected to the next" and family history as "a biographical study of a genealogically proven family and of the community and country in which they lived".<sup id="cite_ref-sog_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sog-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Motivation">Motivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Motivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Individuals conduct genealogical research for a number of reasons.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_or_medical_interest">Personal or medical interest</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Personal or medical interest">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Private individuals do genealogy out of curiosity about their heritage. This curiosity can be particularly strong among those whose family histories were lost or unknown due to, for example, <a href="/wiki/Adoption" title="Adoption">adoption</a> or separation from family through divorce, death, or other situations.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> In addition to simply wanting to know more about who they are and where they came from, individuals may research their genealogy to learn about any <a href="/wiki/Family_history_(medicine)" title="Family history (medicine)">hereditary diseases</a> in their family history.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a growing interest in family history in the media as a result of advertising and television shows sponsored by large genealogy companies such as <a href="/wiki/Ancestry.com" title="Ancestry.com">Ancestry.com</a>. This coupled with easier access to online records and the affordability of <a href="/wiki/DNA_tests" class="mw-redirect" title="DNA tests">DNA tests</a> has both inspired curiosity and allowed those who are curious to easily start investigating their ancestry.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Community_or_religious_obligation">Community or religious obligation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Community or religious obligation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Collective_identity" title="Collective identity">communitarian</a> societies, one's identity is defined as much by one's kin network as by individual achievement, and the question "Who are you?" would be answered by a description of father, mother, and tribe. New Zealand <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_people" title="Māori people">Māori</a>, for example, learn <a href="/wiki/Whakapapa" title="Whakapapa">whakapapa</a> (genealogies) to discover who they are.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Family history plays a part in the practice of some religious belief systems. For example, <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a> (LDS Church) has a doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Baptism_for_the_dead" title="Baptism for the dead">baptism for the dead</a>, which necessitates that members of that faith engage in family history research.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/East_Asian_cultural_sphere" title="East Asian cultural sphere">East Asian</a> countries that were historically shaped by <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>, many people follow a practice of <a href="/wiki/Ancestor_worship" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancestor worship">ancestor worship</a> as well as genealogical record-keeping. Ancestor's names are inscribed on <a href="/wiki/Spirit_tablet" title="Spirit tablet">tablets</a> and placed in shrines, where rituals are performed. Genealogies are also recorded in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_kin#Zupu—the_genealogical_book" title="Chinese kin">genealogy books</a>. This practice is rooted in the belief that <a href="/wiki/Filial_piety" title="Filial piety">respect for one's family</a> is a foundation for a healthy society.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishing_identity">Establishing identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Establishing identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Royal_families" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal families">Royal families</a>, both historically and in modern times, keep records of their genealogies in order to establish their <a href="/wiki/Right_to_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Right to rule">right to rule</a> and determine who will be the next sovereign. For centuries in various cultures, ones genealogy has been a source of political and social status.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some countries and indigenous tribes allow individuals to obtain citizenship based on their genealogy. In <a href="/wiki/Irish_nationality_law#By_descent" title="Irish nationality law">Ireland</a>, for example, an individual can become a citizen if one of their grandparents was born in Ireland, even if the individual or their parents were not born there. In societies such as Australia or the United States, there was by the 20th century growing pride in the pioneers and nation-builders. Establishing descent from these was, and is, important to <a href="/wiki/Lineage_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Lineage society">lineage societies</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Daughters_of_the_American_Revolution" title="Daughters of the American Revolution">Daughters of the American Revolution</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Mayflower_Society" class="mw-redirect" title="The Mayflower Society">The Mayflower Society</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>  Modern family history explores new sources of status, such as celebrating the resilience of families that survived generations of poverty or slavery, or the success of families in integrating across racial or national boundaries. Some family histories even emphasize links to celebrity criminals, such as the bushranger <a href="/wiki/Ned_Kelly" title="Ned Kelly">Ned Kelly</a> in Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_and_forensic_research">Legal and forensic research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Legal and forensic research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Forensic_genealogy" title="Forensic genealogy">Forensic genealogy</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lawyers" class="mw-redirect" title="Lawyers">Lawyers</a> involved in <a href="/wiki/Probate" title="Probate">probate cases</a> do genealogy to locate heirs of property.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Detectives" class="mw-redirect" title="Detectives">Detectives</a> may perform genealogical research using <a href="/wiki/DNA_evidence" class="mw-redirect" title="DNA evidence">DNA evidence</a> to identify victims of <a href="/wiki/Homicides" class="mw-redirect" title="Homicides">homicides</a> or perpetrators of crimes.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scholarly_research">Scholarly research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Scholarly research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Historians" class="mw-redirect" title="Historians">Historians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geneticists" class="mw-redirect" title="Geneticists">geneticists</a> may do genealogical research to gain a greater understanding of specific topics in their respective fields.
Professional genealogists conduct paid genealogical research for any of the above individuals. They also publish their research in peer-reviewed journals.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:P._63_genealogy_and_history_from_Adam_and_Eve_to_Asclobitotus_(unfinished).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/P._63_genealogy_and_history_from_Adam_and_Eve_to_Asclobitotus_%28unfinished%29.jpg/220px-P._63_genealogy_and_history_from_Adam_and_Eve_to_Asclobitotus_%28unfinished%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="298" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/P._63_genealogy_and_history_from_Adam_and_Eve_to_Asclobitotus_%28unfinished%29.jpg/330px-P._63_genealogy_and_history_from_Adam_and_Eve_to_Asclobitotus_%28unfinished%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/P._63_genealogy_and_history_from_Adam_and_Eve_to_Asclobitotus_%28unfinished%29.jpg/440px-P._63_genealogy_and_history_from_Adam_and_Eve_to_Asclobitotus_%28unfinished%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="592" data-file-height="802" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:P._63_genealogy_and_history_from_Adam_and_Eve_to_Asclobitotus_(unfinished).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Medieval genealogy traced from <a href="/wiki/Adam_and_Eve" title="Adam and Eve">Adam and Eve</a></div></div></div>
<p>Historically, in Western societies the focus of genealogy was on the <a href="/wiki/Kinship_and_descent" class="mw-redirect" title="Kinship and descent">kinship and descent</a> of rulers and nobles, often arguing or demonstrating the legitimacy of claims to wealth and power. The term often overlapped with <a href="/wiki/Heraldry" title="Heraldry">heraldry</a>, in which the ancestry of royalty was reflected in their <a href="/wiki/Coats_of_arms" class="mw-redirect" title="Coats of arms">coats of arms</a>. Modern scholars consider many claimed noble ancestries to be fabrications, such as the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Chronicle" title="Anglo-Saxon Chronicle">Anglo-Saxon Chronicle</a> that traced the ancestry of several English kings to the god <a href="/wiki/Woden" class="mw-redirect" title="Woden">Woden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
Some family trees have been maintained for considerable periods.  The family tree of <a href="/wiki/Confucius" title="Confucius">Confucius</a> has been maintained for over 2,500 years and is listed in the <a href="/wiki/Guinness_Book_of_Records" class="mw-redirect" title="Guinness Book of Records">Guinness Book of Records</a> as the largest extant family tree. The fifth edition of the <a href="/wiki/Confucius_Genealogy" class="mw-redirect" title="Confucius Genealogy">Confucius Genealogy</a> was printed in 2009 by the <a href="/wiki/Confucius_Genealogy_Compilation_Committee" title="Confucius Genealogy Compilation Committee">Confucius Genealogy Compilation Committee</a> (CGCC).<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_times">Modern times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Modern times">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In modern times, genealogy became more widespread, with commoners as well as nobility researching and maintaining their family trees.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Genealogy received a boost in the late 1970s with the television broadcast of <i><a href="/wiki/Roots:_The_Saga_of_an_American_Family" title="Roots: The Saga of an American Family">Roots: The Saga of an American Family</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Alex_Haley" title="Alex Haley">Alex Haley</a>. His account of his family's descent from the African tribesman <a href="/wiki/Kunta_Kinte" title="Kunta Kinte">Kunta Kinte</a> inspired many others to study their own lines.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>With the advent of the <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a>, the number of resources readily accessible to genealogists has vastly increased, resulting in an explosion of interest in the topic.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>  Genealogy is one of the most popular topics on the Internet.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The Internet has become not only a major source of data for genealogists, but also of education and communication.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In India, <a href="/wiki/Charan" title="Charan">Charans</a> are the <a href="/wiki/Bard" title="Bard">Bards</a> who traditionally keep the written genealogy records of various <a href="/wiki/Caste" title="Caste">castes</a>. Some notable places where traditional genealogy records are kept include: <a href="/wiki/Hindu_genealogy_registers_at_Haridwar" title="Hindu genealogy registers at Haridwar">Hindu genealogy registers at Haridwar (Uttarakhand)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Varanasi" title="Varanasi">Varanasi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Allahabad" title="Allahabad">Allahabad</a> (<a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>), <a href="/wiki/Hindu_genealogy_registers_at_Kurukshetra,_Haryana" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu genealogy registers at Kurukshetra, Haryana">Kurukshetra (Haryana)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trimbakeshwar_Shiva_Temple" title="Trimbakeshwar Shiva Temple">Trimbakeshwar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Maharashtra" title="Maharashtra">Maharashtra</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Chintpurni" title="Chintpurni">Chintpurni</a> (<a href="/wiki/Himachal_Pradesh" title="Himachal Pradesh">Himachal Pradesh</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-gene1_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gene1-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Genealogical research in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> was first systematized in the early 19th century, especially by <a href="/wiki/John_Farmer_(author)" title="John Farmer (author)">John Farmer</a> (1789–1838).<sup id="cite_ref-weil_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weil-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Before Farmer's efforts, tracing one's genealogy was seen as an attempt by colonists to secure a measure of social standing within the British Empire, an aim that was counter to the new republic's egalitarian, future-oriented ethos.<sup id="cite_ref-weil_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weil-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> As Fourth of July celebrations commemorating the Founding Fathers and the heroes of the Revolutionary War became increasingly popular, however, the pursuit of 'antiquarianism,' which focused on local history, became acceptable as a way to honor the achievements of early Americans.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Farmer capitalized on the acceptability of antiquarianism to frame genealogy within the early republic's ideological framework of pride in one's American ancestors. He corresponded with other antiquarians in New England, where antiquarianism and genealogy were well established, and became a coordinator, booster, and contributor to the growing movement. In the 1820s, he and fellow antiquarians began to produce genealogical and antiquarian tracts in earnest, slowly gaining a devoted audience among the American people. Though Farmer died in 1839, his efforts led to the creation of the <a href="/wiki/New_England_Historic_Genealogical_Society" title="New England Historic Genealogical Society">New England Historic Genealogical Society</a> (NEHGS), one of New England's oldest and most prominent organizations dedicated to the preservation of public records.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> NEHGS publishes the <i>New England Historical and Genealogical Register.</i>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Genealogical_Society_of_Utah" class="mw-redirect" title="Genealogical Society of Utah">Genealogical Society of Utah</a>, founded in 1894, later became the Family History Department of the LDS Church. The department's research facility, the <a href="/wiki/Family_History_Library" title="Family History Library">Family History Library</a>, which Utah.com states is "the largest genealogical library in the world,"<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> was established to assist in tracing family lineages for special religious ceremonies which Latter-day Saints believe will seal family units together for eternity. Latter-day Saints believe that this fulfilled a biblical prophecy stating that the prophet <a href="/wiki/Elijah" title="Elijah">Elijah</a> would return to "turn the heart of the fathers to the children, and the heart of the children to their fathers."<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> There is a network of church-operated <a href="/wiki/Family_History_Center_(LDS_Church)" title="Family History Center (LDS Church)">Family History Centers</a> all over the country and around the world, where volunteers assist the public with tracing their ancestors.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brigham_Young_University" title="Brigham Young University">Brigham Young University</a> offers bachelor's degree, minor, and concentration programs in Family History, and is the only school in North America to offer this.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Society_of_Genealogists" title="American Society of Genealogists">American Society of Genealogists</a> is the scholarly honorary society of the U.S. genealogical field. Founded by John Insley Coddington, Arthur Adams, and Meredith B. Colket, Jr., in December 1940, its membership is limited to 50 living fellows. ASG publishes <a href="/wiki/The_Genealogist" title="The Genealogist">The Genealogist</a>, a scholarly journal of genealogical research semi-annually since 1980. <a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_American_Society_of_Genealogists" title="Fellow of the American Society of Genealogists">Fellow of the American Society of Genealogists</a>, who bear the post-nominal acronym FASG, have written some of the most notable genealogical materials of the last half-century.<sup id="cite_ref-FASG_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FASG-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some of the most notable scholarly American genealogical journals are <i><a href="/wiki/The_American_Genealogist" title="The American Genealogist">The American Genealogist</a></i>, <i>National Genealogical Society Quarterly</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_England_Historical_and_Genealogical_Register" class="mw-redirect" title="The New England Historical and Genealogical Register">The New England Historical and Genealogical Register</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Genealogical_and_Biographical_Record" title="The New York Genealogical and Biographical Record">The New York Genealogical and Biographical Record</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Genealogist" title="The Genealogist">The Genealogist</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_process">Research process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Research process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Genealogical research is a complex process that uses historical records and sometimes genetic analysis to demonstrate kinship. Reliable conclusions are based on the quality of sources, ideally original records, the information within those sources, ideally primary or firsthand information, and the evidence that can be drawn, directly or indirectly, from that information. In many instances, genealogists must skillfully assemble indirect or <a href="/wiki/Circumstantial_evidence" title="Circumstantial evidence">circumstantial evidence</a> to build a case for identity and kinship. All evidence and conclusions, together with the documentation that supports them, is then assembled to create a cohesive genealogy or <a href="/wiki/Family_history" class="mw-redirect" title="Family history">family history</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Genealogists begin their research by collecting family documents and stories. This creates a foundation for <a href="/wiki/Documentary_research" title="Documentary research">documentary research</a>, which involves examining and evaluating historical records for evidence about ancestors and other relatives, their kinship ties, and the events that occurred in their lives. As a rule, genealogists begin with the present and work backward in time. Historical, social, and family context is essential to achieving correct identification of individuals and relationships. Source citation is also important when conducting genealogical research.<sup id="cite_ref-evidence_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-evidence-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> To keep track of collected material, family group sheets and <a href="/wiki/Pedigree_chart" title="Pedigree chart">pedigree charts</a> are used. Formerly handwritten, these can now be generated by genealogical software.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_analysis">Genetic analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Genetic analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genetic_genealogy" title="Genetic genealogy">Genetic genealogy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:D1S80Demo.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/D1S80Demo.png/220px-D1S80Demo.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/D1S80Demo.png/330px-D1S80Demo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/18/D1S80Demo.png 2x" data-file-width="361" data-file-height="200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:D1S80Demo.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Variations of <a href="/wiki/VNTR" class="mw-redirect" title="VNTR">VNTR</a> allele lengths in 6 individuals</div></div></div>
<p>Because a person's <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> contains information that has been passed down relatively unchanged from early ancestors, <a href="/wiki/Genetic_fingerprinting" class="mw-redirect" title="Genetic fingerprinting">analysis of DNA</a> is sometimes used for genealogical research. Three DNA types are of particular interest: <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> that we all possess and that is passed down with only minor mutations through the matrilineal (direct female) line; the <a href="/wiki/Y-chromosome" class="mw-redirect" title="Y-chromosome">Y-chromosome</a>, present only in males, which is passed down with only minor mutations through the patrilineal (direct male) line; and the <a href="/wiki/Autosomal_DNA" class="mw-redirect" title="Autosomal DNA">Autosomal DNA</a>, which is found in the 22 non-gender specific chromosomes (autosomes) inherited from both parents, which can uncover relatives from any branch of the family.  A <a href="/wiki/Genealogical_DNA_test" title="Genealogical DNA test">genealogical DNA test</a> allows two individuals to find the probability that they are, or are not, related within an estimated number of generations. Individual <a href="/wiki/Genealogical_DNA_test" title="Genealogical DNA test">genetic test results</a> are collected in databases to match people descended from a relatively recent common ancestor. See, for example, the <a href="/wiki/Molecular_Genealogy_Research_Project" class="mw-redirect" title="Molecular Genealogy Research Project">Molecular Genealogy Research Project</a>. These tests are limited to either the patrilineal or the matrilineal line.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Collaboration">Collaboration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Collaboration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_software" title="Genealogy software">genealogy software</a> programs can export information about persons and their relationships in a standardized format called <a href="/wiki/GEDCOM" title="GEDCOM">GEDCOM</a>. In that format it can be shared with other genealogists, added to databases, or converted into family web sites. <a href="/wiki/Social_networking_service" title="Social networking service">Social networking service</a> (SNS) websites allow genealogists to share data and build their family trees online. Members can upload their family trees and contact other family historians to fill in gaps in their research.  In addition to the (SNS) websites, there are other resources that encourage genealogists to connect and share information such as rootsweb.ancestry.com<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> and rsl.rootsweb.ancestry.com.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Volunteerism">Volunteerism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Volunteerism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Volunteerism" class="mw-redirect" title="Volunteerism">Volunteer efforts</a> figure prominently in genealogy.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> These range from the extremely informal to the highly organized.
</p><p>On the informal side are the many popular and useful <a href="/wiki/Internet_forum" title="Internet forum">message boards</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Rootschat" title="Rootschat">Rootschat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electronic_mailing_list" title="Electronic mailing list">mailing lists</a> on particular surnames, regions, and other topics. These forums can be used to try to find relatives, request record lookups, obtain research advice, and much more. Many genealogists participate in loosely organized projects, both online and off. These collaborations take numerous forms. Some projects prepare name indexes for records, such as <a href="/wiki/Probate" title="Probate">probate</a> cases, and publish the indexes, either online or off. These indexes can be used as <a href="/wiki/Finding_aid" title="Finding aid">finding aids</a> to locate original records. Other projects transcribe or abstract records. Offering record lookups for particular geographic areas is another common service. Volunteers do record lookups or take photos in their home areas for researchers who are unable to travel.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Those looking for a structured volunteer environment can join one of thousands of <a href="/wiki/Genealogical_societies" class="mw-redirect" title="Genealogical societies">genealogical societies</a> worldwide. Most societies have a unique area of focus, such as a particular surname, <a href="/wiki/Ethnicity" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnicity">ethnicity</a>, geographic area, or <a href="/wiki/List_of_hereditary_%26_lineage_organizations" class="mw-redirect" title="List of hereditary &amp; lineage organizations">descendancy from participants in a given historical event</a>. Genealogical societies are almost exclusively staffed by volunteers and may offer a broad range of services, including maintaining libraries for members' use, publishing newsletters, providing research assistance to the public, offering classes or seminars, and organizing record preservation or transcription projects.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Software">Software</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Software">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fan-chart-example-gramps5.0.1win10.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Fan-chart-example-gramps5.0.1win10.png/220px-Fan-chart-example-gramps5.0.1win10.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Fan-chart-example-gramps5.0.1win10.png/330px-Fan-chart-example-gramps5.0.1win10.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Fan-chart-example-gramps5.0.1win10.png/440px-Fan-chart-example-gramps5.0.1win10.png 2x" data-file-width="1159" data-file-height="791" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fan-chart-example-gramps5.0.1win10.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gramps" title="Gramps">Gramps</a> is an example of genealogy software.</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_software" title="Genealogy software">Genealogy software</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Genealogy_software" title="Genealogy software">Genealogy software</a> is used to collect, store, sort, and display genealogical data. At a minimum, genealogy software accommodates basic information about individuals, including births, marriages, and deaths. Many programs allow for additional biographical information, including occupation, residence, and notes, and most also offer a method for keeping track of the sources for each piece of evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
Most programs can generate basic kinship charts and reports, allow for the import of digital photographs and the export of data in the <a href="/wiki/GEDCOM" title="GEDCOM">GEDCOM</a> format (short for GEnealogical Data COMmunication) so that data can be shared with those using other genealogy software. More advanced features include the ability to restrict the information that is shared, usually by removing information about living people out of <a href="/wiki/Privacy" title="Privacy">privacy</a> concerns; the import of sound files; the generation of family history books, web pages and other publications; the ability to handle <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriages</a> and children born out of wedlock; searching the Internet for data; and the provision of research guidance.  Programs may be geared toward a specific religion, with fields relevant to that religion, or to specific nationalities or ethnic groups, with source types relevant for those groups. Online resources involve complex programming and large data bases, such as censuses.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Records_and_documentation">Records and documentation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Records and documentation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Family-bible-births.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Family-bible-births.jpg/220px-Family-bible-births.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Family-bible-births.jpg/330px-Family-bible-births.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Family-bible-births.jpg/440px-Family-bible-births.jpg 2x" data-file-width="952" data-file-height="761" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Family-bible-births.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A family history page from an <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Southern_United_States#Antebellum_era" title="History of the Southern United States">antebellum era</a> family Bible</div></div></div>
<p>Genealogists use a wide variety of records in their research. To effectively conduct genealogical research, it is important to understand how the records were created, what information is included in them, and how and where to access them.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_record_types">List of record types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: List of record types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Records that are used in genealogy research include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Vital_records" class="mw-redirect" title="Vital records">Vital records</a>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Birth_certificate" title="Birth certificate">Birth records</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Death_certificate" title="Death certificate">Death records</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Marriage_license" title="Marriage license">Marriage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Divorce" title="Divorce">divorce</a> records</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adoption" title="Adoption">Adoption</a> records</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Biographies" class="mw-redirect" title="Biographies">Biographies</a> and biographical profiles (e.g. <i><a href="/wiki/Marquis_Who%27s_Who" title="Marquis Who&#39;s Who">Who's Who</a></i>)</li>
<li>Cemetery lists</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Census" title="Census">Census</a> records</li>
<li>Religious records
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">Baptism</a> or <a href="/wiki/Infant_baptism" title="Infant baptism">christening</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Brit_milah" title="Brit milah">Brit milah</a> or <a href="/wiki/Baby_naming" class="mw-redirect" title="Baby naming">Baby naming</a> certificates</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Confirmation" title="Confirmation">Confirmation</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bar_mitzvah" class="mw-redirect" title="Bar mitzvah">Bar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bat_mitzvah" class="mw-redirect" title="Bat mitzvah">bat mitzvah</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Marriage" title="Marriage">Marriage</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Funeral" title="Funeral">Funeral</a> or death</li>
<li>Membership</li></ul></li>
<li>City directories<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Telephone_directory" title="Telephone directory">telephone directories</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Coroner" title="Coroner">Coroner</a>'s reports</li>
<li>Court records
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Criminal_record" title="Criminal record">Criminal records</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Civil_records" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil records">Civil records</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Diary" title="Diary">Diaries</a>, personal <a href="/wiki/Letter_(message)" title="Letter (message)">letters</a> and family <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bibles</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Genealogical_DNA_test" title="Genealogical DNA test">DNA tests</a><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">Emigration</a>, <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Naturalization" title="Naturalization">naturalization</a> records</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_hereditary_%26_lineage_organizations" class="mw-redirect" title="List of hereditary &amp; lineage organizations">Hereditary &amp; lineage organization</a> records, e.g. <a href="/wiki/Daughters_of_the_American_Revolution" title="Daughters of the American Revolution">Daughters of the American Revolution</a> records</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Real_property" title="Real property">Land</a> and property records, <a href="/wiki/Deed" title="Deed">deeds</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">Medical</a> records</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Armed_force" class="mw-redirect" title="Armed force">Military</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">conscription</a> records</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Newspaper" title="Newspaper">Newspaper</a> articles</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Obituary" title="Obituary">Obituaries</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Profession" title="Profession">Occupational</a> records</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oral_histories" class="mw-redirect" title="Oral histories">Oral histories</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Passport" title="Passport">Passports</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Photograph" title="Photograph">Photographs</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Poorhouse" title="Poorhouse">Poorhouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Workhouse" title="Workhouse">workhouse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Almshouse" title="Almshouse">almshouse</a>, and asylum records</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/School" title="School">School</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alumni_association" title="Alumni association">alumni association</a> records</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Passenger_ship" title="Passenger ship">Ship</a> passenger lists</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Social_Security_(United_States)" title="Social Security (United States)">Social Security</a> (within the US) and <a href="/wiki/Pension" title="Pension">pension</a> records</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tax" title="Tax">Tax</a> records</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tombstone" class="mw-redirect" title="Tombstone">Tombstones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cemetery" title="Cemetery">cemetery</a> records, and <a href="/wiki/Funeral_home" title="Funeral home">funeral home</a> records</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vote" class="mw-redirect" title="Vote">Voter</a> registration records</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Will_(law)" class="mw-redirect" title="Will (law)">Wills</a> and <a href="/wiki/Probate" title="Probate">probate</a> records</li></ul>
<p>To keep track of their citizens, governments began keeping <a href="/wiki/Public_records" title="Public records">records</a> of persons who were neither <a href="/wiki/Royal_family" title="Royal family">royalty</a> nor <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">nobility</a>. In England and Germany, for example, such record keeping started with parish registers in the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> As more of the population was recorded, there were sufficient records to follow a family. Major life events, such as births, marriages, and deaths, were often documented with a license, permit, or report. Genealogists locate these records in local, regional or national offices or <a href="/wiki/Archive" title="Archive">archives</a> and extract information about family relationships and recreate <a href="/wiki/Chronology" title="Chronology">timelines</a> of persons' lives.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and other Asian countries, <a href="/wiki/Genealogy_book" title="Genealogy book">genealogy books</a> are used to record the names, occupations, and other information about family members, with some books dating back hundreds or even thousands of years. In the eastern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> state of <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>, there is a written tradition of genealogical records among Maithil Brahmins and Karna Kayasthas called "<a href="/wiki/Panjis" title="Panjis">Panjis</a>", dating to the 12th century CE. Even today these records are consulted prior to marriages.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, genealogical records were recorded by professional families of <i>senchaidh</i> (historians) until as late as the mid-17th century. Perhaps the most outstanding example of this genre is <a href="/wiki/Leabhar_na_nGenealach" title="Leabhar na nGenealach">Leabhar na nGenealach</a>/<a href="/wiki/The_Great_Book_of_Irish_Genealogies" class="mw-redirect" title="The Great Book of Irish Genealogies">The Great Book of Irish Genealogies</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Dubhaltach_MacFhirbhisigh" class="mw-redirect" title="Dubhaltach MacFhirbhisigh">Dubhaltach MacFhirbhisigh</a> (d. 1671), published in 2004.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="FamilySearch_collections">FamilySearch collections</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: FamilySearch collections">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LDS_genealogy_library_slc_utah.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/LDS_genealogy_library_slc_utah.jpg/220px-LDS_genealogy_library_slc_utah.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/LDS_genealogy_library_slc_utah.jpg/330px-LDS_genealogy_library_slc_utah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/LDS_genealogy_library_slc_utah.jpg/440px-LDS_genealogy_library_slc_utah.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LDS_genealogy_library_slc_utah.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Family History Library, operated by The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-Day Saints, is the world's largest library dedicated to genealogical research.</div></div></div>
<p>The LDS Church has engaged in large-scale microfilming of records of genealogical value. Its Family History Library in Salt Lake City, Utah, houses over 2 million microfiche and microfilms of genealogically relevant material, which are also available for on-site research at over 4500 <a href="/wiki/Family_History_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Family History Center">Family History Centers</a> worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/FamilySearch" title="FamilySearch">FamilySearch</a>'s website includes many resources for genealogists: a FamilyTree database, historical records,<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> digitized family history books,<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> resources and indexing for African American genealogy such as slave and bank records,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Family_History_Research_Wiki" title="Family History Research Wiki">Family History Research Wiki</a> containing research guidance articles.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indexing_ancestral_Information">Indexing ancestral Information</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Indexing ancestral Information">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Indexing is the process of transcribing parish records, city vital records, and other reports, to a digital database for searching.  Volunteers and professionals participate in the indexing process.  Since 2006, the microfilm in the FamilySearch granite mountain vault is in the process of being digitally scanned, available online, and eventually indexed.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For example, after the 72-year legal limit for releasing personal information for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census" title="United States Census">United States Census</a> was reached in 2012, genealogical groups cooperated to index the 132 million residents registered in the <a href="/wiki/1940_United_States_Census" title="1940 United States Census">1940 United States Census</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 2006 and 2012, the FamilySearch indexing effort produced more than 1 billion searchable records.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Record_loss_and_preservation">Record loss and preservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Record loss and preservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sometimes genealogical records are destroyed, whether accidentally or on purpose. In order to do thorough research, genealogists keep track of which records have been destroyed so they know when information they need may be missing. Of particular note for North American genealogy is the <a href="/wiki/1890_United_States_Census" title="1890 United States Census">1890 United States Census</a>, which was destroyed in a fire in 1921. Although fragments survive, most of the 1890 census no longer exists. Those looking for genealogical information for families that lived in the United States in 1890 must rely on other information to fill that gap.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>War is another cause of record destruction. During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, many European records were destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-familysearch_preservation_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-familysearch_preservation-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Communists in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Cultural_Revolution" title="Cultural Revolution">Cultural Revolution</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> destroyed genealogy books kept by families.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Often records are destroyed due to accident or neglect. Since genealogical records are often kept on paper and stacked in high-density storage, they are prone to fire, mold, insect damage, and eventual disintegration. Sometimes records of genealogical value are deliberately destroyed by governments or organizations because the records are considered to be unimportant or a privacy risk. Because of this, genealogists often organize efforts to preserve records that are at risk of destruction. <a href="/wiki/FamilySearch" title="FamilySearch">FamilySearch</a> has an ongoing program that assesses what useful genealogical records have the most risk of being destroyed, and sends volunteers to digitize such records.<sup id="cite_ref-familysearch_preservation_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-familysearch_preservation-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> In 2017, the government of <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a> asked FamilySearch for help preserving their rapidly deteriorating vital records. FamilySearch has begun digitizing the records and making them available online.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Genealogical_Societies" title="Federation of Genealogical Societies">Federation of Genealogical Societies</a> also organized an effort to preserve and digitize United States <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> pension records. In 2010, they began raising funds, which were contribute by genealogists around the United States and matched by <a href="/wiki/Ancestry.com" title="Ancestry.com">Ancestry.com</a>. Their goal was achieved and the process of digitization was able to begin. The digitized records are available for free online.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_information">Types of information</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Types of information">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Genealogists who seek to reconstruct the lives of each ancestor consider all historical information to be "genealogical" information. Traditionally, the basic information needed to ensure correct identification of each person are place names, occupations, <a href="/wiki/Family_name" class="mw-redirect" title="Family name">family names</a>, first names, and dates. However, modern genealogists greatly expand this list, recognizing the need to place this information in its historical context in order to properly evaluate genealogical evidence and distinguish between same-name individuals. A great deal of information is available for British ancestry<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> with growing resources for other ethnic groups.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family_names">Family names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Family names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lineage_scroll_of_Mary_Fane.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Lineage_scroll_of_Mary_Fane.jpg/220px-Lineage_scroll_of_Mary_Fane.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="316" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Lineage_scroll_of_Mary_Fane.jpg/330px-Lineage_scroll_of_Mary_Fane.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Lineage_scroll_of_Mary_Fane.jpg/440px-Lineage_scroll_of_Mary_Fane.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2206" data-file-height="3172" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lineage_scroll_of_Mary_Fane.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lineage of a family, c1809</div></div></div>
<p>Family names are simultaneously one of the most important pieces of genealogical information, and a source of significant confusion for researchers.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In many cultures, the name of a person refers to the family to which he or she belongs. This is called the <i><a href="/wiki/Family_name" class="mw-redirect" title="Family name">family name</a></i>, <i>surname</i>, or <i>last name</i>. <a href="/wiki/Patronymic" title="Patronymic">Patronymics</a> are names that identify an individual based on the father's name. For example, Marga Olafsdottir is Marga, daughter of Olaf, and Olaf Thorsson is Olaf, son of Thor. Many cultures used patronymics before surnames were adopted or came into use. The Dutch in New York, for example, used the patronymic system of names until 1687 when the advent of English rule mandated surname usage.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> In Iceland, patronymics are used by a majority of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> In Denmark and Norway patronymics and farm names were generally in use through the 19th century and beyond, though surnames began to come into fashion toward the end of the 19th century in some parts of the country. Not until 1856 in Denmark<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> and 1923 in Norway<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> were there laws requiring surnames.
</p><p>The transmission of names across generations, marriages and other relationships, and immigration may cause difficulty in genealogical research. For instance, women in many cultures have routinely used their spouse's surnames. When a woman remarried, she may have changed her name and the names of her children; only her name; or changed no names. Her birth name (<a href="/wiki/Maiden_name" class="mw-redirect" title="Maiden name">maiden name</a>) may be reflected in her children's middle names; her own middle name; or dropped entirely.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> Children may sometimes assume stepparent, foster parent, or adoptive parent names. Because official records may reflect many kinds of surname change, without explaining the underlying reason for the change, the correct identification of a person recorded identified with more than one name is challenging.  Immigrants to America often Americanized their names.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Surname data may be found in trade directories, census returns, birth, death, and marriage records.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Given_names">Given names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Given names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">April 2015</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Genealogical data regarding <a href="/wiki/Given_name" title="Given name">given names</a> (first names) is subject to many of the same problems as are family names and place names. Additionally, the use of <a href="/wiki/Nicknames" class="mw-redirect" title="Nicknames">nicknames</a> is very common. For example, Beth, Lizzie or Betty are all common for Elizabeth, and Jack, John and Jonathan may be interchanged.
</p><p>Middle names provide additional information. Middle names may be inherited, follow naming customs, or be treated as part of the family name. For instance, in some Latin cultures, both the mother's family name and the father's family name are used by the children.
</p><p>Historically, naming traditions existed in some places and cultures. Even in areas that tended to use naming conventions, however, they were by no means universal. Families may have used them some of the time, among some of their children, or not at all. A pattern might also be broken to name a newborn after a recently deceased sibling, aunt or uncle.
</p><p>An example of a naming tradition from England, Scotland and Ireland:
</p>
<div style="text-align:center;">
<table width="50%">
<tbody><tr>
<td><b>Child</b>
</td>
<td><b>Namesake</b>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>1st son
</td>
<td>paternal grandfather
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>2nd son
</td>
<td>maternal grandfather
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>3rd son
</td>
<td>father
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>4th son
</td>
<td>father's oldest brother
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>1st daughter
</td>
<td>maternal grandmother
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>2nd daughter
</td>
<td>paternal grandmother
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>3rd daughter
</td>
<td>mother
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>4th daughter
</td>
<td>mother's oldest sister
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div>
<p>Another example is in some areas of Germany, where siblings were given the same first name, often of a favourite saint or local nobility, but different second names by which they were known (<i>Rufname</i>). If a child died, the next child of the same gender that was born may have been given the same name. It is not uncommon that a list of a particular couple's children will show one or two names repeated.
</p><p>Personal names have periods of popularity, so it is not uncommon to find many similarly named people in a generation, and even similarly named families; e.g., "William and Mary and their children David, Mary, and John".
</p><p>Many names may be identified strongly with a particular gender; e.g., William for boys, and Mary for girls. Others may be <a href="/wiki/Unisex_names" class="mw-redirect" title="Unisex names">ambiguous</a>, e.g., Lee, or have only slightly variant spellings based on gender, e.g., Frances (usually female) and Francis (usually male).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Place_names">Place names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Place names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While the locations of ancestors' residences and life events are core elements of the genealogist's quest, they can often be confusing. Place names may be subject to variant spellings by partially literate scribes. Locations may have identical or very similar names. For example, the village name <a href="/wiki/Brockton_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Brockton (disambiguation)">Brockton</a> occurs six times in the border area between the English counties of <a href="/wiki/Shropshire" title="Shropshire">Shropshire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire" title="Staffordshire">Staffordshire</a>. Shifts in political borders must also be understood. Parish, county, and national borders have frequently been modified. Old records may contain references to farms and villages that have ceased to exist. When working with older records from Poland, where borders and place names have changed frequently in past centuries, a source with maps and sample records such as <i><a href="/wiki/A_Translation_Guide_to_19th-Century_Polish-Language_Civil-Registration_Documents" title="A Translation Guide to 19th-Century Polish-Language Civil-Registration Documents">A Translation Guide to 19th-Century Polish-Language Civil-Registration Documents</a></i> can be invaluable.
</p><p>Available sources may include vital records (civil or church registration), censuses, and tax assessments. Oral tradition is also an important source, although it must be used with caution. When no source information is available for a location, circumstantial evidence may provide a probable answer based on a person's or a family's place of residence at the time of the event.
</p><p>Maps and gazetteers are important sources for understanding the places researched. They show the relationship of an area to neighboring communities and may be of help in understanding migration patterns. <a href="/wiki/Family_tree_mapping" title="Family tree mapping">Family tree mapping</a> using online mapping tools such as <a href="/wiki/Google_Earth" title="Google Earth">Google Earth</a> (particularly when used with Historical Map overlays such as those from the <a href="/wiki/David_Rumsey_Historical_Map_Collection" title="David Rumsey Historical Map Collection">David Rumsey Historical Map Collection</a>) assist in the process of understanding the significance of geographical locations.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dates">Dates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Dates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It is wise to exercise extreme caution with dates. Dates are more difficult to recall years after an event, and are more easily mistranscribed than other types of genealogical data.<sup id="cite_ref-knights_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knights-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, one should determine whether the date was recorded at the time of the event or at a later date. Dates of birth in vital records or civil registrations and in church records at baptism are generally accurate because they were usually recorded near the time of the event. Family Bibles are often a source for dates, but can be written from memory long after the event. When the same ink and handwriting is used for all entries, the dates were probably written at the same time and therefore will be less reliable since the earlier dates were probably recorded well after the event. The publication date of the Bible also provides a clue about when the dates were recorded since they could not have been recorded at any earlier date.
</p><p>People sometimes reduce their age on marriage, and those under "full age" may increase their age in order to marry or to join the armed forces. <a href="/wiki/Census" title="Census">Census</a> returns are notoriously unreliable for ages or for assuming an approximate death date. Ages over 15 in the <a href="/wiki/UK_Census" class="mw-redirect" title="UK Census">1841 census in the UK</a> are rounded down to the next lower multiple of five years.
</p><p>Although baptismal dates are often used to approximate birth dates, some families waited years before baptizing children, and adult baptisms are the norm in some religions. Both birth and marriage dates may have been adjusted to cover for pre-wedding pregnancies.
</p><p>Calendar changes must also be considered. In 1752, England and her American colonies changed from the <a href="/wiki/Julian_calendar" title="Julian calendar">Julian</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>. In the same year, the date the new year began was changed. Prior to 1752 it was <a href="/wiki/Lady_Day" title="Lady Day">25 March</a>; this was changed to 1 January. Many other European countries had already made the calendar changes before England had, sometimes centuries earlier. By 1751 there was an 11-day discrepancy between the date in England and the date in other European countries.
</p><p>For further detail on the changes involved in moving from the Julian to the Gregorian calendar, see: <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/French_Republican_Calendar" class="mw-redirect" title="French Republican Calendar">French Republican Calendar</a> or French Revolutionary Calendar was a calendar proposed during the French Revolution, and used by the French government for about 12 years from late 1793 to 1805, and for 18 days in 1871 in Paris. Dates in official records at this time use the revolutionary calendar and need "translating" into the Gregorian calendar for calculating ages etc. There are various websites which do this.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occupations">Occupations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Occupations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Occupational information may be important to understanding an ancestor's life and for distinguishing two people with the same name. A person's occupation may have been related to his or her social status, political interest, and migration pattern. Since skilled trades are often passed from father to son, occupation may also be indirect evidence of a family relationship.
</p><p>It is important to remember that a person may change occupations, and that titles change over time as well. Some workers no longer fit for their primary trade often took less prestigious jobs later in life, while others moved upwards in prestige.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Many unskilled ancestors had a variety of jobs depending on the season and local trade requirements. Census returns may contain some embellishment; e.g., from <a href="/wiki/Labourer" class="mw-redirect" title="Labourer">labourer</a> to <a href="/wiki/Masonry" title="Masonry">mason</a>, or from <a href="/wiki/Journeyman" title="Journeyman">journeyman</a> to <a href="/wiki/Master_craftsman" title="Master craftsman">master craftsman</a>. Names for old or unfamiliar local occupations may cause confusion if poorly legible. For example, an ostler (a keeper of horses) and a hostler (an innkeeper) could easily be confused for one another. Likewise, descriptions of such occupations may also be problematic. The perplexing description "ironer of rabbit burrows" may turn out to describe an ironer (profession) in the <a href="/wiki/Bristol" title="Bristol">Bristol</a> district named Rabbit Burrows.
