Several trades have regionally preferred terms. For example, "shoemaker" and "cordwainer" have the same meaning. Finally, many apparently obscure jobs are part of a larger trade community, such as watchmaking, <a href="/wiki/Stocking_frame" title="Stocking frame">framework knitting</a> or gunmaking.
</p><p>Occupational data may be reported in occupational licenses, tax assessments, membership records of professional organizations, trade directories, census returns, and vital records (civil registration). Occupational dictionaries are available to explain many obscure and archaic trades.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reliability_of_sources">Reliability of sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Reliability of sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Information found in historical or genealogical sources can be unreliable and it is good practice to evaluate all sources with a critical eye. Factors influencing the reliability of genealogical information include: the knowledge of the informant (or writer); the bias and mental state of the informant (or write; the passage of time and the potential for copying and compiling errors.
</p><p>The quality of census data has been of special interest to historians, who have investigated reliability issues.<sup id="cite_ref-knights_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knights-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Knowledge_of_the_informant">Knowledge of the informant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Knowledge of the informant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The informant is the individual who provided the recorded information. Genealogists must carefully consider who provided the information and what he or she knew. In many cases the informant is identified in the record itself. For example, a death certificate usually has two informants: a physician who provides information about the time and cause of death and a family member who provides the birth date, names of parents, etc.
</p><p>When the informant is not identified, one can sometimes deduce information about the identity of the person by careful examination of the source. One should first consider who was alive (and nearby) when the record was created. When the informant is also the person recording the information, the handwriting can be compared to other handwriting samples.
</p><p>When a source does not provide clues about the informant, genealogists should treat the source with caution. These sources can be useful if they can be compared with independent sources. For example, a census record by itself cannot be given much weight because the informant is unknown. However, when censuses for several years concur on a piece of information that would not likely be guessed by a neighbor, it is likely that the information in these censuses was provided by a family member or other informed person. On the other hand, information in a single census cannot be confirmed by information in an undocumented compiled genealogy since the genealogy may have used the census record as its source and might therefore be dependent on the same misinformed individual.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Motivation_of_the_informant">Motivation of the informant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Motivation of the informant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Even individuals who had knowledge of the fact, sometimes intentionally or unintentionally provided false or misleading information. A person may have lied in order to obtain a government benefit (such as a military pension), avoid taxation, or cover up an embarrassing situation (such as the existence of a non-marital child). A person with a distressed state of mind may not be able to accurately recall information. Many genealogical records were recorded at the time of a loved one's death, and so genealogists should consider the effect that grief may have had on the informant of these records.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_effect_of_time">The effect of time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: The effect of time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The passage of time often affects a person's ability to recall information. Therefore, as a general rule, data recorded soon after the event are usually more reliable than data recorded many years later. However, some types of data are more difficult to recall after many years than others. One type especially prone to recollection errors is dates. Also the ability to recall is affected by the significance that the event had to the individual. These values may have been affected by cultural or individual preferences.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Copying_and_compiling_errors">Copying and compiling errors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Copying and compiling errors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Genealogists must consider the effects that copying and compiling errors may have had on the information in a source. For this reason, sources are generally categorized in two categories: original and derivative. An original source is one that is not based on another source. A derivative source is information taken from another source. This distinction is important because each time a source is copied, information about the record may be lost and errors may result from the copyist misreading, mistyping, or miswriting the information. Genealogists should consider the number of times information has been copied and the types of derivation a piece of information has undergone. The types of derivatives include: photocopies, transcriptions, abstracts, translations, extractions, and compilations.
</p><p>In addition to copying errors, compiled sources (such as published genealogies and online pedigree databases) are susceptible to misidentification errors and incorrect conclusions based on circumstantial evidence. Identity errors usually occur when two or more individuals are assumed to be the same person. Circumstantial or indirect evidence does not explicitly answer a genealogical question, but either may be used with other sources to answer the question, suggest a probable answer, or eliminate certain possibilities. Compilers sometimes draw hasty conclusions from circumstantial evidence without sufficiently examining all available sources, without properly understanding the evidence, and without appropriately indicating the level of uncertainty.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_and_secondary_sources">Primary and secondary sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Primary and secondary sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In genealogical research, information can be obtained from primary or secondary sources. Primary sources are records that were made at the time of the event, for example a death certificate would be a primary source for a person's death date and place. Secondary sources are records that are made days, weeks, months, or even years after an event.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Standards_and_Ethics">Standards and Ethics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Standards and Ethics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Organizations that educate and certify genealogists have established standards and ethical guidelines they instruct genealogists to follow.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_standards">Research standards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Research standards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Genealogy research requires analyzing documents and drawing conclusions based on the evidence provided in the available documents. Genealogists need standards to determine whether or not their evaluation of the evidence is accurate. In the past, genealogists in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> borrowed terms from <a href="/wiki/Burden_of_proof_(law)" title="Burden of proof (law)">judicial law</a> to examine evidence found in documents and how they relate to the researcher's conclusions. However, the differences between the two disciplines created a need for genealogists to develop their own standards. In 2000, the <a href="/wiki/Board_for_Certification_of_Genealogists" title="Board for Certification of Genealogists">Board for Certification of Genealogists</a> published their first manual of standards. The <a href="/wiki/Genealogical_Proof_Standard" title="Genealogical Proof Standard">Genealogical Proof Standard</a> created by the Board for Certification of Genealogists is widely distributed in seminars, workshops, and educational materials for genealogists in the United States. Other genealogical organizations around the world have created similar standards they invite genealogists to follow. Such standards provide guidelines for genealogists to evaluate their own research as well as the research of others.
</p><p>Standards for genealogical research include:<sup id="cite_ref-bcgstandards_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bcgstandards-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sogstandards_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sogstandards-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iajgs_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iajgs-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Clearly document and organize findings.</li>
<li>Cite all sources in a specific manner so that others can locate them and properly evaluate them.</li>
<li>Locate all available sources that may contain information relevant to the research question.</li>
<li>Analyze findings thoroughly, without ignoring conflicts in records or negative evidence.</li>
<li>Rely on original, rather than derivative sources, wherever possible.</li>
<li>Use logical reasoning based on reliable sources to reach conclusions.</li>
<li>Acknowledge when a specific conclusion is only "possible" or "probable" rather than "proven."</li>
<li>Acknowledge that other records that have not yet been discovered may overturn a conclusion.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethical_guidelines">Ethical guidelines</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Genealogy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Ethical guidelines">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Genealogists often handle sensitive information and share and publish such information. Because of this, there is a need for ethical standards and boundaries for when information is too sensitive to be published. Historically, some genealogists have fabricated information or have otherwise been untrustworthy. Genealogical organizations around the world have outlined ethical standards as an attempt to eliminate such problems. Ethical standards adopted by various genealogical organizations include:<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-iajgs_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iajgs-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sogstandards_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sogstandards-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Respect copyright laws</li>
<li>Acknowledge where one consulted another's work and do not plagiarize the work of other researchers.</li>
<li>Treat original records with respect and avoid causing damage to them or removing them from repositories.</li>
<li>Treat archives and archive staff with respect.</li>
<li>Protect the privacy of living individuals by not publishing or otherwise disclosing information about them without their permission.</li>
<li>Disclose any conflicts of interest to clients.</li>
<li>When doing paid research, be clear with the client about scope of research and fees involved.</li>
<li>Do not fabricate information or publish false or unproven information as proven.</li>
<li>Be sensitive about information found through genealogical research that may make the client or family members uncomfortable.</li></ul>
<p>In 2015, a committee presented standards for <a href="/wiki/Genetic_genealogy" title="Genetic genealogy">genetic genealogy</a> at the Salt Lake Institute of Genealogy. The standards emphasize that genealogists and testing companies should respect the privacy of clients and recognize the limits of DNA tests. It also discusses how genealogists should thoroughly document conclusions made using DNA evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> In 2019, the Board for the Certification of Genealogists officially updated their standards and code of ethics to include standards for genetic genealogy.<sup id="cite_ref-bcgstandards_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bcgstandards-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Te Rito, Joseph. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.review.mai.ac.nz/mrindex/MR/article/view/56/55.html">"Whakapapa: A framework for understanding identity"</a>. <i>MAI Review</i>. <b>2007</b> (2). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190624214114/http://www.review.mai.ac.nz/mrindex/MR/article/view/56/55.html">Archived</a> from the original on 24 June 2019<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://newsroom.churchofjesuschrist.org/topic/genealogy">"Topic:Genealogy"</a>. <i>The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints: Newsroom</i>. The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190624200254/https://newsroom.churchofjesuschrist.org/topic/genealogy">Archived</a> from the original on 24 June 2019<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">24 June</span> 2018</span>. <q>Latter-day Saints believe that the eternal joining of families is possible through sacred sealing ceremonies that take place in temples. These temple rites may also be performed by proxy for those who have died. Consequently, for Latter-day Saints, genealogical research or family history is the essential forerunner for temple work for the dead. In Latter-day Saint belief, the dead have the choice to accept or reject the services performed for them.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=The+Church+of+Jesus+Christ+of+Latter-day+Saints%3A+Newsroom&amp;rft.atitle=Topic%3AGenealogy&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fnewsroom.churchofjesuschrist.org%2Ftopic%2Fgenealogy&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGenealogy" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-17">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.abc.net.au/news/2018-07-05/why-does-the-mormon-church-want-state-records/9943288">"Why does the Mormon Church want state records? And what do they do with them?"</a>. <i>ABC News Australia</i>. 4 Jul 2018. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180708033341/http://www.abc.net.au/news/2018-07-05/why-does-the-mormon-church-want-state-records/9943288">Archived</a> from the original on 8 July 2018<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">24 June</span> 2018</span>. <q>Members of the church use family history records to perform sacred temple ordinances, such as baptisms, eternal marriages and sealings of children to parents, for their kindred dead if the deceased family members were unable to perform the rites themselves. This gives deceased ancestors the opportunity to accept these ordinances in the afterlife.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=ABC+News+Australia&amp;rft.atitle=Why+does+the+Mormon+Church+want+state+records%3F+And+what+do+they+do+with+them%3F&amp;rft.date=2018-07-04&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.abc.net.au%2Fnews%2F2018-07-05%2Fwhy-does-the-mormon-church-want-state-records%2F9943288&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGenealogy" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-81">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation news"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://news.google.com/newspapers?id=EdFSAAAAIBAJ&amp;sjid=IH8DAAAAIBAJ&amp;pg=5788%2C3311192">"Genealogy Important In Korea, Expert Says"</a>. <i>The Deseret News</i>. 13 Sep 1975<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Surnames made their way into the language in the 19th and 20th century, but are not widely used.
